<div id="review_1848202625" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1848202625" />
    <a title="Alexia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55506205-alexia"><img alt="Alexia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524198481p2/55506205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1848202625?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexia" href="/user/show/55506205-alexia">Alexia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55506205-alexia?shelf=favs">favs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55506205-alexia?shelf=swoonworthy-heroes">swoonworthy-heroes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55506205-alexia?shelf=cant-get-enough-of-it">cant-get-enough-of-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55506205-alexia?shelf=witty">witty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55506205-alexia?shelf=chemistry">chemistry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55506205-alexia?shelf=angst">angst</a>,

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1848202625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18354544669840453204">update 26/feb/2017: there are so many grammatical mistakes and typos in here, darn it.<br/>please bear with me if you spot them. i'm too lazy to correct them. tyvm.<br/><br/>One of the two books directly responsible for the end of my chick lit reading frenzy.<br/>This book is 100% chick lit. The plot is not original, as is the case for 99% of the genre, but-the characters sure as hell are.<br/><br/><b>Jessica Darling</b> is, upon first glance, your typical honors-heroine. The kind that is supposedly brainy and cynical and usually g</span>
  <span id="freeText18354544669840453204" style="display:none">update 26/feb/2017: there are so many grammatical mistakes and typos in here, darn it.<br />please bear with me if you spot them. i'm too lazy to correct them. tyvm.<br /><br />One of the two books directly responsible for the end of my chick lit reading frenzy.<br />This book is 100% chick lit. The plot is not original, as is the case for 99% of the genre, but-the characters sure as hell are.<br /><br /><b>Jessica Darling</b> is, upon first glance, your typical honors-heroine. The kind that is supposedly brainy and cynical and usually goes about slut-shaming everybody but themselves. Put simply, annoying little hypocrites. Now Jessica is definitely a hypocrite, but she's more or less cognizant of it. Okay, maybe she isn't, but she makes it up for being absolutely hilarious in her internal monologues. Also, she <i>does</i> seem genuinely intelligent. Lots of times heroines in chick lit are straight-A students or e.t.c, but besides from the occasional SAT word (that means, unless you are Hazel/Augustus you won't ever use these words in speech, because unless you are using it to annoy someone, it's not all that useful) there's nothing else to support the fact that they are what they claim to be. Jessica's manifestation of her IQ isn't impulsive. It's consistent. And for this "Show, not tell", she earns a million brownie points.<br /><br /><b>Marcus Flutie</b> is, upon first impression, the typical bad-boy. But he rejects that erroneous assumption nearly immediately, by appearing too mysterious for that. However, he's never quite demystified in this book, and for that reason he makes everything much more interesting. The readers can decipher his actions without too much restraint, and I love that. Personally, (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[and this was proven to be mostly correct in the 2nd installment <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) I like to think of Marcus as a hopeless born romantic who just doesn't give a shit about being successful because life is too short and he simply wants to live happily in his own way. (<i>YES!</i> you got it goin', lad!) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Oh, but he originally did drugs and smoked a bit. However, he did it less for the readers' benefit (meaning, it wasn't just there to assert his bad-boyness) and more for his own amusement, so it's less of a stain on his character. More importantly, he quit in the end. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I loved how McCafferty dealt with Hope and Bridget. Jessica spent 50% of the book lamenting over the loss of her best friend, Hope, who moved away from Pinesville, but Hope was conspicuously absent throughout the book. She was more of a symbol of hope than anything, and I found the concept of this very unique and cool. <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Bridget was portrayed as a brainless cheerleader for 80% of the book. However, instead of going on about how clueless Bridget is and how Jessica is better than her, McCafferty chose to redeem Bridget, explaining how she may not be that bright but isn’t a complete loser either. That’s how I like it. Since most chick lit specializes in 2-dimensional characters (among the most popular of these cardboard-cuts are 1. the bitch and 2. the idiot), this was very refreshing for me.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It really irks me how girls in chick lit are often Regina George or Karen Smith: in reality nobody has that flat a character.<br /><br />Now for the complaints. <br /><br />I didn’t care much for the author’s treatment of Scotty. He started off as a really cute, really unique character. Jocks in books are usually just one of two types of jerks: a) the obnoxious, egotistical moron b) the hot, ex-man-whore who reformed because of the heroine. At first, Scotty fell into neither category. He was just a goofy sort of boy who just happened to like Jessica. He didn’t go around shoving his masculinity into everybody’s face. But he jock-alizes, becoming His Royal Guyness. I get that the author did it on purpose, putting emphasis on how easy it is to lose yourself under the pressure from the expectation of your peers, but I would’ve loved <i>Sloppy Firsts</i> even more if Scotty had remained true to himself. At first I was even rooting for Scotty.<br />If Scotty had wound up with Jessica, and if Bridget had become Jessica’s best friend, this would’ve been a true original, a “classic” as Marcus would call it. Why? This book would’ve been a tale of how a self-righteous, arrogant honors girl came to dispose of her condescension towards those not as brilliant as her. It would be about how she finally relinquished her pride and prejudice. <b>Most importantly, this book would have succeeded in rejecting the biggest chick-lit tropes known to man.</b> I just <i>know</i> that if the author tried to this, she <i>would</i> have been able to pull it off without turning this book into a complete preaching.<br />The only problem with this proposal is that I wouldn't have gotten to know Marcus. However, I am willing (!) to not know Marcus (!!) if it means that McCafferty makes <i>Sloppy Firsts</i> into the one and only chick lit that takes me off guard.<br /><br /><b>But she didn’t.</b><br /><br />And that’s why this book is pure chick-lit. But that’s also fine, since I picked this up expecting a fluffy journey, and that’s precisely what I got. A very finely written one, I must add. I’m just saying that, <i>if</i> the author had done what I said, I would’ve been truly, truly blown away.<br /><br /><b>5 stars</b> for outdoing any other angsty teen documentary.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18354544669840453204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_4231746" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4231746" />
    <a title="Jennie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48469-jennie"><img alt="Jennie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1176263996p2/48469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4231746?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennie" href="/user/show/48469-jennie">Jennie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48469-jennie?shelf=nonrequired-work-reading">nonrequired-work-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4231746" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1900861908738994445">Jessica Darling is anything but darling–so much so that her father calls her Notso. Jessica Notso Darling. She hates her friends, except for Hope, who just moved from New Jersey to Tennessee. But she’s halfway through her sophomore year of high school without anyone to talk to except the Clueless Crew and maybe the new girl, Hy.<br/><br/>She hates her family–her sister and mother are completely wrapped up in Bethany’s upcoming wedding and expect Jessica to be as well. They don’t understand why she wouldn’</span>
  <span id="freeText1900861908738994445" style="display:none">Jessica Darling is anything but darling–so much so that her father calls her Notso. Jessica Notso Darling. She hates her friends, except for Hope, who just moved from New Jersey to Tennessee. But she’s halfway through her sophomore year of high school without anyone to talk to except the Clueless Crew and maybe the new girl, Hy.<br /><br />She hates her family–her sister and mother are completely wrapped up in Bethany’s upcoming wedding and expect Jessica to be as well. They don’t understand why she wouldn’t want to take Scott as her date, he’s so cute! Never mind that they shared an awful first kiss two years ago and Scott is still pining after her and she just doesn’t like him that way. Jessica’s father is obsessed with her running career, follows her on his bike when she runs and makes video montages of all the races she’s lost so they can analyze what she did wrong. He takes it as a personal insult when she breaks her leg one night and is out for the rest of the season.<br /><br />And then there’s Marcus, the class junkie, who keeps showing up wherever Jessica is and knows way too much about her. Marcus, whom she can’t tell Hope about, knowing Hope would disapprove. Marcus, who got her to pee in a cup to fake a drug test. Marcus, who shouldn’t effect her they way he does. Marcus, who might be the best thing since Hope left...<br /><br />Biting, funny and well written, this is not standard chick-lit fare. Jessica’s journal entries and monthly letters to Hope tell a story that rings true and entertains without being sappy or melodramatic. I also liked this book because it gets lumped in the YA chick-lit section but really isn't... I know my library actually originally cataloged it as an adult title, and it really is. This is a great book for teens who read the teen lit stuff and want to bridge to more serious stuff (and contains lots of good vocab words). This is also a great book for adults... now I'm just babbling. It's been a long night.<br /><br />see all reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.tushuguan.blogspot.com">http://www.tushuguan.blogspot.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1900861908738994445" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_121124950" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121124950" />
    <a title="YA Reads Book Reviews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1764232-ya-reads-book-reviews"><img alt="YA Reads Book Reviews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285429046p2/1764232.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121124950?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YA Reads Book Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YA Reads Book Reviews" href="/user/show/1764232-ya-reads-book-reviews">YA Reads Book Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121124950" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1452511079041718160">This is not a book about teen pregnancy. This is not a book that advocates or condemns teen pregnancy. This is a book about Jessica Darling. Jess is many things – a runner, a daughter, an academic, a sister, and a friend – but pregnant is certainly not one of them. No sir. In order to be pregnant one would have to engage in sexual intercourse first, and that would require actually snaking the attention of her high school crush – Paul Parlipiano. But even though they run on the same track team Pa</span>
  <span id="freeText1452511079041718160" style="display:none">This is not a book about teen pregnancy. This is not a book that advocates or condemns teen pregnancy. This is a book about Jessica Darling. Jess is many things – a runner, a daughter, an academic, a sister, and a friend – but pregnant is certainly not one of them. No sir. In order to be pregnant one would have to engage in sexual intercourse first, and that would require actually snaking the attention of her high school crush – Paul Parlipiano. But even though they run on the same track team Paul doesn’t even know she exists. So pregnancy, my dear friends, is so far from the reason Jess’s period is MIA it’s not even funny.<br /><br />The real explanation is far more boring, far less scandalous, far more Jessica. Jess, you see, is a runner. She’s on the track team and she trains a lot. She has trouble sleeping and she’s really skinny. Even after her grueling training sessions, when she can’t sleep in the middle of the night, Jess gets up and goes running, hoping that she’ll tire herself out so she can sleep. She runs and runs and runs and runs. Jess knows this is the reason she hasn’t got her period, and although she knows its bad to go for so long without menstruating, her running seems to be a bigger priority.<br /><br />Then one night, while out running, Jess injures her ankle. She manages to drag herself home limping and crying and moaning in pain. Exit excessive running here. In fact, exit all running here. At least now her dad can’t bug her about her track meets anymore, not being able to actually participate and all. But what about the middle of the night? How is she going to get to sleep now?<br /><br />Enter Marcus Flutie. He’s the resident bad boy, the one everyone loves to hate. Turns out that he and Jess have far more in common than she originally thought – not that she was thinking of him, no sir, someone like Jessica Darling would never be thinking of someone like Marcus Flutie – and talking to Marcus in the middle of the night seems to be the only way Jess can get herself to sleep. Jess learns many things about Marcus, but the most important of all is that Marcus is so not the person she thought he was. Jess realises that she’s falling for him, but brushes it aside because she knows that Marcus doesn’t date girls like her. Besides, he’s got a floozy girlfriend anyway. She sees them groping in the hallways all the time. Gross. But Jess is beautifully naïve and often doesn’t see what’s right in front of her. Will Marcus lead good, wholesome Jess down a path of disobedience and destruction, like everyone thinks he will? And honestly, how can she stand him anyway? No one gets it… no one at all.<br /><br />Jessica’s voice is raw and fresh. Megan McCafferty captures what so many teens are thinking but do not say. Jessica Darling is so real that she could be me, or you, or the girl sitting next to you on the bus. I bought every single word that came out of her mouth. Her way of looking at things brings light and humour to even the saddest and darkest of situations, and makes for one hell of an entertaining read. You’ll laugh, you might cry, but you’ll certainly feel every pang, every stomach churn that good ol’ Jess goes through. As her name suggests, she’s every bit the teenage darling.<br /><br />One of the best books I’ve read in a long time. I positively cannot wait to get my hands on a copy of the next book in the series, Second Helpings.<br /><br />P.S I think I’ve come down with a case of Flutie Fever. Watch out, I hear it’s very contagious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1452511079041718160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5874251-ashley?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5874251-ashley?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5874251-ashley?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5874251-ashley?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer298883821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11843294681806898892">This is one of my favourite YA books. I've always loved Jessica's cynicism, and the mystery of Marcus Flutie. And I totally love the ending. Even though I've read this book so many times, I think I will always continue to enjoy it. It's funny, it's cute, it's relatable.<br/><br/><b>Edit 11/18/16 -</b> Just to expand on my kind of paltry review, this is one of my favourite books, period. I love the way it's written, not just the journal format, but Jessica's voice. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/79922.Megan_McCafferty' title='Megan McCafferty' rel='nofollow noopener'> Megan McCafferty</a> wrote a teenage girl in the ea</span>
  <span id="freeText11843294681806898892" style="display:none">This is one of my favourite YA books. I've always loved Jessica's cynicism, and the mystery of Marcus Flutie. And I totally love the ending. Even though I've read this book so many times, I think I will always continue to enjoy it. It's funny, it's cute, it's relatable.<br /><br /><b>Edit 11/18/16 -</b> Just to expand on my kind of paltry review, this is one of my favourite books, period. I love the way it's written, not just the journal format, but Jessica's voice. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/79922.Megan_McCafferty" title="Megan McCafferty" rel="nofollow noopener"> Megan McCafferty</a> wrote a teenage girl in the early 00's to a point. (Reading it now, near the end of 2016, I was howling at the mention of butterfly clips, body glitter, and platform Mary Janes. I <b>adored</b> my platform MJs!) <br /><br />It surprises me that after so many reads over so many years:<br />1) I still get totally engrossed in this book. Even though I can probably recite the who book word-for-word, I still am totally submerged in the story. I read every word, I don't skim anything, and I just get lost in the book every single time.<br />2) I still find this book funny. Again, because of the amazing authorship, and the way the words are written, there are a lot of parts that make me chuckle. Jessica's conversations, her wry observations. Bethany and Pepe and the Clueless Crew, and Jessica's interactions with them - just so much gold in one book.<br />3) Marcus Flutie still makes me swoon. From the strange first encounters, to the beginning of their friendship, it all still makes me smile. And their outing to the diner (and the Marcusmarcusmarcus<i>marred kiss</i>) still makes my heart flutter. <br /><br />This book is always a delight to read. I'm sure most reviews say this, but it's like Jessica is an old friend, so it's always nice to go back and read her story. I'd recommend this to anybody ever, because it is definitely one of my favourite books I have ever read.<br /><br /><b>Edit 7/27/20 -</b> Hmm, after this read I wasn't so enchanted by this book, for first time ever. There's an awful lot of slut-shaming. And I felt like the Marcus thing wasn't as great as I remembered, but that could have been because I was thinking of stuff that happens in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/199687.Second_Helpings__Jessica_Darling___2_" title="Second Helpings (Jessica Darling, #2) by Megan McCafferty" rel="nofollow noopener"> Second Helpings</a>
</i>. It was still a very readable book, and I'm sure looking back on my own diaries when I was 16 (if I wrote in diaries) would be this cringey too. Am I growing up?!?!?!?!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11843294681806898892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vmUmPvSIigpXDfaVtJjeMvflIy26e614/Uj7UhGgFqqE65m1mMXLJ/ecaxVz6Poh0Qbud24lQWF6AZVYfDukgw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Accepting a dream offer at the prestigious Manhattan law firm of Klasko &amp; Fitch, she promises her sweet and supportive longtime boyfriend that the job won’t change her. Yet Alex is seduced by the firm’s money and energy . . . and by her cocksure male colleagues, who quickly take notice of the new girl. She’s never felt so confident and powerful—even the innuendo-laced banter with clients feels fun. In the firm’s most profitable and competitive division, Mergers and Acquisitions, Alex works around the clock, racking up billable hours and entertaining clients late into the evening. While the job is punishing, it has its perks, like a weekend trip to Miami, a ride in a client’s private jet, and more expense-account meals than she can count. \n\nBut as her clients’ expectations and demands on her increase, and Alex finds herself magnetically drawn to a handsome coworker despite her loving relationship at home, she begins to question everything—including herself. She knows the corporate world isn’t black and white, and that to reach the top means playing by different rules. But who made those rules? And what if the system rigged so that women can’t win, anyway? \n\nWhen something happens that reveals the dark reality of the firm, Alex comes to understand the ways women like her are told—explicitly and implicitly—how they need to behave to succeed in the workplace. And what she describes throughout her short stories are people, and particularly parents, who defy this maxim. For this reason her work is timeless, like all great writing. It is irrelevant that Dorothy Whipple’s novels were set in an era when middle-class women expected to have a maid; when fish knives were used for eating fish; when children did what they were told. The moral universe she creates has not changed: there are bullies in every part of society; people try their best but often fail; they would like to be unselfish but sometimes are greedy.<\/span>\n He became a member of the Black Panther Party while in Angola State Penitentiary, successfully organizing prisoners to improve conditi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12128750288190170505\" style=\"display:none\">In 1970, a jury convicted Robert Hillary King of a crime he did not commit and sentenced him to 35 years in prison. He became a member of the Black Panther Party while in Angola State Penitentiary, successfully organizing prisoners to improve conditions. In return, prison authorities beat him, starved him, and gave him life without parole after framing him for a second crime. He was thrown into solitary confinement, where he remained in a six by nine foot cell for 29 years as one of the Angola 3. In 2001, the state grudgingly acknowledged his innocence and set him free. This is his story.It begins at the beginning: born black, born poor, born in Louisiana in1942, King journeyed to Chicago as a hobo at the age of 15. He married and had a child, and briefly pursued a semi-pro boxing career to help provide for his family. Just a teenager when he entered the Louisiana penal system for the first time, King tells of his attempts to break out of this system, and his persistent pursuit of justice where there is none.Yet this remains a story of inspiration and courage, and the triumph of the human spirit. The conditions in Angola almost defy description, yet King never gave up his humanity, or the work towards justice for all prisoners that he continues to do today. From the Bottom of the Heap, so simply and humbly told, strips bare the economic and social injustices inherent in our society, while continuing to be a powerful literary testimony to our own strength <span id="freeTextContainer1075747310068060792">Megan McCafferty writes fiction for tweens, teens and teens-at-heart of all ages. The author of eleven novels, she’s best known for SLOPPY FIRSTS and four more sequels in the New York Times bestselling Jessica Darling series. She's got two new books coming out in 2020: TRUE TO YOUR SELFIE (MG, Scholastic, 2/20) and THE MALL (YA, Wednesday Books, 6/20). Described in her first review as “Judy Blume </span>
  <span id="freeText1075747310068060792" style="display:none">Megan McCafferty writes fiction for tweens, teens and teens-at-heart of all ages. The author of eleven novels, she’s best known for SLOPPY FIRSTS and four more sequels in the New York Times bestselling Jessica Darling series. She's got two new books coming out in 2020: TRUE TO YOUR SELFIE (MG, Scholastic, 2/20) and THE MALL (YA, Wednesday Books, 6/20). Described in her first review as “Judy Blume meets Dorothy Parker” (Wall Street Journal), she’s been trying to live up to that high standard ever since.</span>
